However, if you have specific constraints, such as an array of integers within a limited range, you can use counting sort or radix sort to achieve linear time complexity. Here's a code snippet using counting sort:\n\n```python\ndef counting_sort(arr):\n    if not arr:\n        return arr\n    max_val = max(arr)\n    min_val = min(arr)\n    range_of_elements = max_val - min_val + 1\n    count = [0] * range_of_elements\n    output = [0] * len(arr)\n\n    for number in arr:\n        count[number - min_val] += 1\n\n    for i in range(1, range_of_elements):\n        count[i] += count[i - 1]\n\n    for number in reversed(arr):\n        output[count[number - min_val] - 1] = number\n        count[number - min_val] -= 1\n\n    return output\n\n# Example usage\narray = [4, 2, 2, 8\nYour output: \n"}], 'max_tokens': 36}

HMMs are generative models that define joint probability distributions for observed and hidden states, assuming observations are generated from hidden states. They are suited for scenarios where state transitions and emissions are probabilistic. MEMMs, on the other hand, are discriminative models that directly model conditional probabilities of state sequences given observations, allowing for richer feature representation and handling overlapping features more efficiently. MEMMs focus on conditional dependencies, making them more flexible than HMMs in leveraging contextual information.\nYour output: \n'}], 'max_tokens': 36}

Calculate the mean of the list.\n2. Subtract the mean from each element to find deviations.\n3. Square each deviation.\n4. Sum the squared deviations.\n5. Divide by \\( n - 1 \\) (where \\( n \\) is the number of elements) to get the variance.\n6. Experiments have consistently violated Bell inequalities, indicating that particles are in undefined, or superposed, states before measurement, and that non-local effects are crucial.
